  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_07e50da6-17bd-47b7-baaf-cbe27391f16f'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8083550230927143160">With <b>House Of Glass</b> comes the final chapter of Pramoedya's epic quartet, set in the Dutch East Indies at the turn of the century. A novel of heroism, passion, and betrayal, it provides a spectacular conclusion to a series hailed as one of the great works of modern literature. At the start of <b>House of Glass</b>, Minke, writer and leader of the dissident movement, is now impriso</span>
  <span id="freeText8083550230927143160" style="display:none">With <b>House Of Glass</b> comes the final chapter of Pramoedya's epic quartet, set in the Dutch East Indies at the turn of the century. A novel of heroism, passion, and betrayal, it provides a spectacular conclusion to a series hailed as one of the great works of modern literature. At the start of <b>House of Glass</b>, Minke, writer and leader of the dissident movement, is now imprisoned--and the narrative has switched to Pangemanann, a former policeman, who has the task of spying and reporting on those who continue the struggle for independence. But the hunter is becoming the hunted. Pangemanann is a victim of his own conscience and has come to admire his adversaries. He must decide whether the law is to safeguard the rights of the people or to control the people. He fears the loss of his position, his family, and his self-respect. At last Pangemanann sees that his true opponents are not Minke and his followers, but rather the dynamism and energy of a society awakened.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8083550230927143160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_400133351" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400133351" />
    <a title="Jed L" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3660669-jed-l"><img alt="Jed L" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407210369p2/3660669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400133351?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jed L" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jed L" href="/user/show/3660669-jed-l">Jed L</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400133351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14508609830546591567">I generally do not read about the topics books are about until after I have finished the book. And it was the same with this quartet. Only after did I read more about Toer, Indonesia, and Tirto Adhi Soerjo (the political figure whom Toer uses as a basis for his main character, Minke). Once I learned more about this man and his work in Indonesia and also saw that so much of it was the same as Minke in the novel I began to understand why Toer did not use Minke as a character in this last book. Ins</span>
  <span id="freeText14508609830546591567" style="display:none">I generally do not read about the topics books are about until after I have finished the book. And it was the same with this quartet. Only after did I read more about Toer, Indonesia, and Tirto Adhi Soerjo (the political figure whom Toer uses as a basis for his main character, Minke). Once I learned more about this man and his work in Indonesia and also saw that so much of it was the same as Minke in the novel I began to understand why Toer did not use Minke as a character in this last book. Instead, he writes from the perspective of a government official, also a native, who works to bring down the political progress Minke strove so hard to achieve in the previous three books. From this perspective, this police officer has great respect for Minke, but care more about his honor, his glory and his position in the colonial culture that he has immersed himself. From this we are able to take away what I think Toer's main point is: that the works of Tirto Adhi Soerjo (Minke) were great, they caused immense change, but they were quickly forgotten and destroyed by the very natives he was trying to help. This book shows how hard it was for Indonesia to begin to move away from colonial power. It was partially difficult because so many natives (especially ones that had some wealth and power) were resistant to anything that would put their wealth and power and prestige in jeopardy, despite the fact that it could be taken away at any time. And in the end, the police officer; Jacques eventually learned this and that is where his journey and the quartet's journey ends.<br /><br />The last book of the Buru Quartet was not a disappointment, but because it left one of my favorite characters of literature-Minke-I did not enjoy it nearly as much. But it still remains a wonderful book. Toer is an amazing writer and while I have mentioned this in previous reviews knowing that this quartet was orally spoken in a political prison camp before being written down and published adds to its richness and soul.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14508609830546591567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Even as he admires and respects Minke, he is responsible for undermining the development of his own people and Indonesia to the benefit of all others, the Europeans and Chinese.<br /><br />Such people are responsible for the power of Europeans and Chinese even today. Very little of the history and culture is available to its people and visitors.</span>

<div id="review_2518385747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2518385747" />

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=southeast-asian-fiction">southeast-asian-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2518385747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2428093041090186082">The rather dark but also satisfying conclusion to Toer's tetralogy, told not from Minke's point of view, but from a native police officer who has bought in to the colonial system, and whose life intersects with Minke's, again and again and again. One comes to the conclusion that Toer intended this to show the eventual selling out of revolutionary values by the bourgeoisie as the curse of Indonesia, which makes sense, given the fact that he was trapped on a prison island when he wrote the damn th</span>
  <span id="freeText2428093041090186082" style="display:none">The rather dark but also satisfying conclusion to Toer's tetralogy, told not from Minke's point of view, but from a native police officer who has bought in to the colonial system, and whose life intersects with Minke's, again and again and again. One comes to the conclusion that Toer intended this to show the eventual selling out of revolutionary values by the bourgeoisie as the curse of Indonesia, which makes sense, given the fact that he was trapped on a prison island when he wrote the damn thing. But really, it's the only way the quartet could end. Anything else would have been national-romantic schmaltz. Toer, genius that he was, was only interested in the real deal.<br /><br />It's hard for me to over-emphasize Toer's importance. For the love of the God I don't believe in, everybody, read his books. They're a voice from another time that is as urgent as ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2428093041090186082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2370152696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2370152696" />
    <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr"><img alt="Moushine Zahr" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457457507p2/53509133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2370152696?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moushine Zahr" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr">Moushine Zahr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2370152696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16432420325744677327">WOW!!! Finally I finished enjoying to read the Quartet Buru by the great Indonesian author Pramoedya Ananta Toer!!! I bought the first 2 novels in English in 2014 in Bali and read them at home the same year. I bought the third book in French language in Lyon in 2018 and at last I ordered the fourth and last book in 2019. What an adventure reading the author's novels!!!<br/><br/>In this last book of the quartet buru, the author surprised his readers with a brand new and fresh angle. While during the first </span>
  <span id="freeText16432420325744677327" style="display:none">WOW!!! Finally I finished enjoying to read the Quartet Buru by the great Indonesian author Pramoedya Ananta Toer!!! I bought the first 2 novels in English in 2014 in Bali and read them at home the same year. I bought the third book in French language in Lyon in 2018 and at last I ordered the fourth and last book in 2019. What an adventure reading the author's novels!!!<br /><br />In this last book of the quartet buru, the author surprised his readers with a brand new and fresh angle. While during the first three books, readers followed the life of Minke from a naive teenager to becoming a pioneer political activist against the social injustice of the colonial governement, in this fourth book, the author introduced a new leading character and narator: M. Jacques Pangemanann. He was only a secondary character who appeared toward the end of the third book and now is the main character of the story. Just like Minke, he's an indigenous well educated in Europe, married to a French woman with children, who rose from a simple inspector arresting local rebels to the expert on indigenous affairs for the central governement. While Minke lived and worked for the indigenous people, Pangemanann lived and worked against them. His character is very well developed throughout the various stages of his career showing all the physical and emotional stress he suffered because of his work and until the end of the novel. <br /><br />Through his eyes like looking down inside a house of glass full of people, readers follow closely:<br />- what happened to Minke just before his exile, <br />- what happened to Minke's entourage and his creations (associations and newspapers), <br />- and who else Minke inspired while in exile and what they created to emulate Minke. <br /><br />This book is also different from the first three because it is a fiction story filled with a detailed political analysis of the various indiginous movements created to claim justice, freedom and independance and the counter actions taken by the colonial powers to oppress these movements and their leaders. However, like his other books, the author wrote his story by including everyone that lives in Indonesia: Javanese, other ethnies from other islands, Muslims and Christians, all sorts of indiginous people, educated and non educated, from the local bourgeoisie or poor peasants, metis, "pure white" Europeans, from netherlands or from other parts of the world, colonial individual and non colonial, chinese local communities and the author never forgot women, who always play major strong roles. <br /><br />This book is different from the first 3 books of the quartet as if written by a different person, but it is still as good as the others. There are differences and similarities between them. The author mentions some of the previous characters and places in this 4th instalment and one of them makes even a surprise and strong appearance at the end. <br /><br />I read 5 novels from the same author and rated them all 5 stars. Pramoedya Ananta Toer is a must read author. I promise myself that in a few years from now, I will re-read the entire quartet buru at once. I hope that I read his other novels too. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16432420325744677327" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_941314857" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/941314857" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30564033-david"><img alt="David" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/941314857?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/30564033-david">David</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer941314857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8876733646972174365">A generally dissatisfying conclusion to an important set of books. There is a new first-person narrator in this fourth and final volume and he is not a success (for me, anyway). My first objection is technical: the two narrative voices are not sufficiently distinguished. Apart from some of the details of character (my second point), the voices are stylistically far too similar. The second objection is to character. Our new narrator is inordinately fond of stressing that, unlike many with a simil</span>
  <span id="freeText8876733646972174365" style="display:none">A generally dissatisfying conclusion to an important set of books. There is a new first-person narrator in this fourth and final volume and he is not a success (for me, anyway). My first objection is technical: the two narrative voices are not sufficiently distinguished. Apart from some of the details of character (my second point), the voices are stylistically far too similar. The second objection is to character. Our new narrator is inordinately fond of stressing that, unlike many with a similar surname, he has two Ns rather than the common Ng ending; the second N stands for Nuremberg. Relentlessly and repeatedly our new narrator stresses how much he loathes the role he is playing in the narrative, yet always excuses his actions as being under orders from superiors and, worse, despite himself, quite frequently expresses taking pleasure in what he also claims he loathes. A third weakness is the narrator’s examination of the novel manuscripts which constitute the first three books of the series, concluding that they are probably not true because some of the names have been changed. The current story is, as confirmed almost at its end, to be understood as the final report to his superiors based on the new narrator’s job as civil servant (following his retirement from the police). He seems incapable of imaging a work of fiction, despite his professed undying admiration and respect for the man he is hounding (under orders, of course) into his grave. <br /><br />If we are meant to sympathize with the new narrator, no reader with a living conscience can. If we are meant to take seriously the moments in which this narrator disparages himself and castigates his weaknesses, then 350+ pages is a long way to go with a ‘Virgil’ who is a self-professed traitor to the principal subject of his writing. <br /><br />There is a subtlety hanging over the whole which makes the latter reading probable, though no more palatable (again, for me). The new narrator speaks often of holding all the other lives with whom he is engaged in a little glass figurine of a house on his desk. The novel is called <i>House of Glass</i>. People who live in (in this case, assume control of) glass houses shouldn’t throw stones.<br /><br />Of the <i>Buru Quartet</i> in general: an important story of a scattered and diverse nation throwing off colonialism, a portrait of an intelligent individual who used knowledge more than force for the good of others, and an indictment of European exploitation of most of the non-European world, especially in the 19th century. Important, but told in a manner that only occasionally invites readers; most of the time, it alienates.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8876733646972174365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2180344939464267779">This was so good. The final chapters are shattering: they give such a powerful and bitter sense of a state agent's wasted life in the service of a morally bankrupt social system, contrasted with the heroic struggles that are erupting against that colonial system. It's hard to sum up, just read it! (but read the first three books first).</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18076120419273354654">Well... with this book I've now completed the <b>Buru Quartet</b>. That was one of my goals for this year. Now I'm wandering aimlessly through my library picking things up at random.<br/><br/>Book four, <i>House of Glass</i>, was different from the first three. The point of view switched from Minke, the main protagonist to a Indies Government official, Meneer Pangemanann, <i>spelled with two 'n's</i>.   <span id="freeText18076120419273354654" style="display:none">Well... with this book I've now completed the <b>Buru Quartet</b>. That was one of my goals for this year. Now I'm wandering aimlessly through my library picking things up at random.<br /><br />Book four, <i>House of Glass</i>, was different from the first three. The point of view switched from Minke, the main protagonist to a Indies Government official, Meneer Pangemanann, <i>spelled with two 'n's</i>. His was a tormented soul with a unlikeable narrative voice.<br /><br />The quartet ended with what read like a history textbook lesson. I was a bit disappointed. I wanted more <i>spirit</i>, more inner dialog from Minke... But remaining true to the first 3 volumes... there was no happy ending.<br /><br /><i>"We all have to accept reality, yes, that's true. But just to accept reality and do nothing else, that is the attitude of human beings who have lost the ability to develop and grow, because human beings also have the ability to create new realities. And if there are no longer people who want to create new realities, then perhaps the word</i> progress <i> should be removed altogether from humankind's vocabulary."<br /><br />-----<br /><br />From an author's writing, I would construct how he felt and thought, what his desires were, weaknesses, what were his particular skills and capabilities, how broad was his general knowledge, and everything was linked together, as if by clear crystal threads. Every piece of writing was a world unto itself, floating halfway between reality and a dream world.</i></span>

<div id="review_1736418370" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1736418370" />
    <a title="SSC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7378810-ssc"><img alt="SSC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606236393p2/7378810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1736418370?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SSC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SSC" href="/user/show/7378810-ssc">SSC</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1736418370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7270190976110667303">Set in colonial Indonesia during the Great War, this book is the last in a series named the Buru Quartet. I have not read the other books.<br/><br/>As a standalone book it works; I'm aware the first three novels provide a back story of nationalism and self determination however the protagonist, Jacques Pangemanann is more than adequate. Talented and complex; Jacques a Medanose native studied at the Sorbonne, rose to high ranks in the police force and was appointed trusted adviser to the colonial governmen</span>
  <span id="freeText7270190976110667303" style="display:none">Set in colonial Indonesia during the Great War, this book is the last in a series named the Buru Quartet. I have not read the other books.<br /><br />As a standalone book it works; I'm aware the first three novels provide a back story of nationalism and self determination however the protagonist, Jacques Pangemanann is more than adequate. Talented and complex; Jacques a Medanose native studied at the Sorbonne, rose to high ranks in the police force and was appointed trusted adviser to the colonial government on native affairs. Minke is his fixation, a journalist who he sends to exile in order to preserve his comforts.<br /><br />The book covers the relations between the colonialists, the natives and the few caught in between, and describes the introduction of European ideals to the Indies. The reason this book is successful is the story of the man and his contradictions and need to preserve the system.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7270190976110667303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2389731961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2389731961" />
    <a title="Carol Chapin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41252532-carol-chapin"><img alt="Carol Chapin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426465457p2/41252532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2389731961?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Chapin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Chapin" href="/user/show/41252532-carol-chapin">Carol Chapin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2389731961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11474289468066931874">I have finally finished this! Often, when I read a book too slowly, I fail to get a good sense of it. Reading in fits and starts can ruin the continuity, both of plot and of impressions. That didn’t seem to happen with this book. Although “House of Glass” covers a period of years and many events, the book is consistent as the internal dialog of the main character.<br/><br/>“House of Glass” is the capstone novel of the Buru Quartet, four books that tell the story of Minke, a native of Java, coming of age i</span>
  <span id="freeText11474289468066931874" style="display:none">I have finally finished this! Often, when I read a book too slowly, I fail to get a good sense of it. Reading in fits and starts can ruin the continuity, both of plot and of impressions. That didn’t seem to happen with this book. Although “House of Glass” covers a period of years and many events, the book is consistent as the internal dialog of the main character.<br /><br />“House of Glass” is the capstone novel of the Buru Quartet, four books that tell the story of Minke, a native of Java, coming of age in the colonial Dutch East Indies. His education, awareness, and growing activism are related in the first three books, all of which he narrates. This book is told from the point of view of Pangemanann, a former policeman who works for the colonial government as an advisor and spy on Native independence movements. <br /><br />Pangemanann is half European and half Menadonese (one of the peoples of the East Indies). He was raised in France, but later moved to the Indies with his French wife. Early in his career, he did his job as a policeman with enthusiasm, but one day realizes that a gang of outlaws that he has defeated were defending the native population from exploitive landowners. He is haunted from that day forward. He is rewarded for his accomplishments by being reassigned to a colonial bureau supporting the Dutch Governor-General. He job is to provide intelligence and recommendations to monitor and control the various nascent organizations working toward Native independence from the Dutch. <br /><br />Pangemanann has conflicting urges: his standing in society due to his position and wealth are extremely important to him. He takes a perverse pride in how well he does his job. But he is constantly torn by the damage he is doing to his own people. <br /><br />At the end of the last book, Minke has been sent into exile. Pangemanann is chosen to escort him into exile. This begins Pangemanann’s obsession with Minke. He lives in Minke’s confiscated house, and studies Minke’s manuscripts – which are the first three novels of the Quartet. All the various political figures that Pangemanann investigates are put into his “House of Glass” for scrutiny. Over the course of the book, he destroys himself with self-loathing. <br /><br />The political atmosphere of this period (1890’s though 1920) is portrayed in detail in the Buru Quartet. Although presented as fiction, I suspect that the events and figures are largely based on real people and happenings. There is so much detail in these books, particularly in “House of Glass”. The books gave me a new perspective on colonialism. Toer discusses the impact of China, Japan, and the Philippines, as well as the competing European countries, on the Indies movements. He even includes a Dutch character, an administrator, who, disillusioned with it all, decides to emigrate to America (the America of 1919!).<br /><br />These four books were a demanding read. I think that the quality of the writing improved throughout the books. The first one felt “odd” to me – a little disjointed and hard to believe at times, but this quickly vanished through the other books. I loved following the growing maturity and awareness of Minke, as well as the growth of the independence movement. “House of Glass” was very easy to read stylistically but chocked with detail and references to characters and organizations from the previous books. I’m sure I won’t remember much of the detail. But I will remember the flavor of the times and the world view presented here. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11474289468066931874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3559289378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3559289378" />
    <a title="Dada" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9969021-dada"><img alt="Dada" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338742167p2/9969021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3559289378?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dada" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dada" href="/user/show/9969021-dada">Dada</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3559289378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1455644917373236175">The last book of the Buru Quartet series written by Pramoedya Ananta Toer, one of Indonesia's most influential writers. Unlike the other 3 books, House of Glass is written with a new & fresh perspective, narrated by M. Jacques Pangemanann, a minor character in the 3rd book who is the mastermind behind the exile of Minke to Moluccas islands. Similar to Minke, Pangemanann is a native who is received European education. But unlike Minke, who used his knowledge and energy to educate, inspire, and le</span>
  <span id="freeText1455644917373236175" style="display:none">The last book of the Buru Quartet series written by Pramoedya Ananta Toer, one of Indonesia's most influential writers. Unlike the other 3 books, House of Glass is written with a new &amp; fresh perspective, narrated by M. Jacques Pangemanann, a minor character in the 3rd book who is the mastermind behind the exile of Minke to Moluccas islands. Similar to Minke, Pangemanann is a native who is received European education. But unlike Minke, who used his knowledge and energy to educate, inspire, and lead the natives toward progression, Pangemanann used it to gaining career achievements by destroying Minke's works. <br /><br />Pangemanann career rose after he had successfully ruining Minke and Sarekat Islam political moves by the end of 3rd book. He started a new role as natives official expert at Algemeen Secretariaat. From his "house of glass", he could see every step was taken by natives who were following Minke's footsteps, and Pangemanann had always succeeded in finding ways to destroy their struggle. Pangemanann is an interesting &amp; well-developed character. As a native himself, he was often facing a dilemma where his conscience and morality were disturbed by his actions, but in the end, he had always chosen his career and position.<br /><br />This book also filled with a detailed political analysis of the various native organization's movements created to claim justice, freedom, and independence and the counter actions taken by the colonial powers to oppress these movements and their leaders. Some of the analysis that I find very interesting is about the fall of Javanese civilizations after the Majapahit kingdom collapsed and the role of women leader in natives independence movements. I also get to know the roots of social issues between natives and Chinese from this book. It was the Dutch colonial government that was instigating natives and Chinese to hate each other in the beginning 20th century in order to prevent these two groups to unite against the Dutch colonial government.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1455644917373236175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1513699700" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1513699700" />
    <a title="Chris Wharton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18899975-chris-wharton"><img alt="Chris Wharton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369066061p2/18899975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1513699700?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Wharton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Wharton" href="/user/show/18899975-chris-wharton">Chris Wharton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=explore-colony-empire">explore-colony-empire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=malay-indo-spore">malay-indo-spore</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=classic-writer-or-work-from-past">classic-writer-or-work-from-past</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=historical-fiction-asia-africa">historical-fiction-asia-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=seasia">seasia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=1-condo-read">1-condo-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18899975-chris-wharton?shelf=third-world">third-world</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1513699700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1166624609719110017">The concluding volume of the Buru Quartet, Toer's tetralogy about the stirrings of anticolonialism against the Dutch in the East Indies in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. After the first three, in which Minke recounts his rise from provincial student to a leader of anti-Dutch thinking and organizing until he is sent into a 5-year internal exile, this one takes an interesting turn to the native policeman Pangemanann, who escorts Minke into exile and then rises to become the only native in</span>
  <span id="freeText1166624609719110017" style="display:none">The concluding volume of the Buru Quartet, Toer's tetralogy about the stirrings of anticolonialism against the Dutch in the East Indies in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. After the first three, in which Minke recounts his rise from provincial student to a leader of anti-Dutch thinking and organizing until he is sent into a 5-year internal exile, this one takes an interesting turn to the native policeman Pangemanann, who escorts Minke into exile and then rises to become the only native in the State Secretariat, the office of the governor-general, in a position near the very top of the colonial internal security service. From there, he tells how he looks into the sprawling archipelago as into "a glass house" he has constructed, watching and countering, with the support of an extensive security apparatus both official and unofficial, the innumerable native organizations and leaders tending toward independence across the islands, at the same time internally conflicted as compromised and betraying the native populations on behalf of his colonial masters. As with Footsteps, the previous volume, there is a fair amount of arguing the various sides of colony- versus-independence issues and discussions of strategies and organizing, but Pangemanann's actions and struggles as a Dostoyevskian sort of character are at the fore, with the broader events unfolding as the subjects of his observations and characters from the earlier novels emerging in memory or fact to haunt or taunt him. In an interesting structural twist and unifying element, Pangemanann is actually in possession of Minke's manuscripts of the earlier novels, which he confiscates during Minke's exile. Full marks to this as the quartet's conclusion and to the quartet as a whole--having just browsed through the previous novels and reread all introductions and afterwords, I am extremely glad I kept up my reading of them over five years.<br /><br />                                                                                       </span>
  <a data-text-id="1166624609719110017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1966804321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1966804321" />
    <a title="Ridzki Samsulhadi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49151928-ridzki-samsulhadi"><img alt="Ridzki Samsulhadi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560038773p2/49151928.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_8868839" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8868839" />
    <a title="Manu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/610021-manu"><img alt="Manu" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8868839?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manu" href="/user/show/610021-manu">Manu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Naeem (really!)</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Manu by: </span>
      Shao Loon Yin
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8868839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5332506640879613690">It has been some time since I read the Buru Quartet. Since my memory of its actual plot points is more than a little hazy, I will begin my noting how I came across this book. I know I gave this book four stars. I think this was because I lacked the intelligence to understand the depth of this book, and so the missing star was a rebuke to my own ignorance.<br/><br/>Prior to reading the Buru Quartet I had recently read Benedict Anderson's "Imagined Communities". Since that book has shaped much of the discus</span>
  <span id="freeText5332506640879613690" style="display:none">It has been some time since I read the Buru Quartet. Since my memory of its actual plot points is more than a little hazy, I will begin my noting how I came across this book. I know I gave this book four stars. I think this was because I lacked the intelligence to understand the depth of this book, and so the missing star was a rebuke to my own ignorance.<br /><br />Prior to reading the Buru Quartet I had recently read Benedict Anderson's "Imagined Communities". Since that book has shaped much of the discussion on postcolonial/anticolonial nationalism and Diaspora studies, I wanted to find something that would compliment the arguments in that book while also highlighting the human texture of the people who fought for Indonesian independence. I was pleasantly surprised when my friend Shao told me about Toer and how the Buru Quartet had, in fact, influenced much of what Anderson wrote in Imagined Communities.<br /><br />The main protagonist is Minke, the son of a feudal landlord in Indonesia. Much to his father's consternation, upon completing his westernized education Minke decides to live in Jakarta and continue with his medical studies - instead of coming back home and joining the family business (as it were).<br /><br />It is here that he is introduced to a woman, Nyai Ontosoroh (a Dutch man's mistress) and her daughter. It is through his contact with this family that he acquires an anti-colonial political consciousness... so, e.g., he starts writing in Malay instead of Dutch, he starts his own newspaper, discovers and reports on the plight of Sugar farmers, etc.<br /><br />Alright, so much for a narrative overview. Nyai Ontosoroh was, at least for me, the most moving and inspiring character in this novel. Even though she was sold to the Dutch man, she is incredibly independent and has oodles of self-respect. It is through watching her that Minke learns of of self-reliance and the possibility of surviving without the colonizer, and indeed, thriving against the colonizer.<br /><br />The other thing that Toer does exceptionally well is highlight the specific economic impact of Dutch colonialism on Indonesian society. <br /><br />In this respect, he does something remarkable. Rather than merely highlight the poverty of the Indonesian countryside, he also populates sections of his book with the most believable characters. For example, he shows that hospitality and the ability to part with everything is the greatest with those who have very little to part with... in a sense, Toer reverses the entire crux of economic thinking. The poor farmers he meets are incredibly generous in their welcome of this man of whom they know nothing. These farmers are willing to go hungry so that there is enough for the guest to eat. Toer shows that sophistication exists precisely in generosity and not in pomposity.<br /><br />I do not want to risk any further description of the book itself. Suffice it to say, I was crying by the end of the first novel, laughing and feeling angry by the second, and inspired throughout.<br /><br />It is also important to note that Toer narrated these books to his fellow prisoners while imprisoned for his political activities against the Suharto regime. These books are as a much a testament to the power of community as it is to the risks of which this community must always beware.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5332506640879613690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2270034733" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2270034733" />
    <a title="Frank B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9826651-frank-b"><img alt="Frank B" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407973151p2/9826651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2270034733?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frank B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank B" href="/user/show/9826651-frank-b">Frank B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2270034733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13749281910610085832">This is my favourite novel in this excellent quartet. Pangemanann is a native policeman, working for the Dutch colonial government. He has the job of monitoring Indonesian nationalist Minke’s activities. At the end of the third book Minke gets banished to Ambon and exits the scene and so Pangemanann takes over from Minke as narrator for the fourth book. He gets promoted to be a colonial official in charge of studying and controlling the subversive movements that Minke led and still inspires. Pan</span>
  <span id="freeText13749281910610085832" style="display:none">This is my favourite novel in this excellent quartet. Pangemanann is a native policeman, working for the Dutch colonial government. He has the job of monitoring Indonesian nationalist Minke’s activities. At the end of the third book Minke gets banished to Ambon and exits the scene and so Pangemanann takes over from Minke as narrator for the fourth book. He gets promoted to be a colonial official in charge of studying and controlling the subversive movements that Minke led and still inspires. Pangemanann, an educated, intelligent man, struggles with his task. He secretly admires Minke and feels sympathy for the cause of his fellow Indonesians (although this term did not yet exist). However, he justifies his mission to destroy Minke’s work: he wants to get his pension and to continue to provide for his French wife and Indo children. His internal conflicts pull him apart, he turns to drink and his wife leaves him. He falls into the arms of a prostitute, becomes ill, but still continues with his duties. Why? Maybe he’s a workaholic, or he knows it can be no other way. I prayed for the author to let him come to a moral decision, to give up his work before it was too late - but I knew it wasn’t going to be. I found Pangemanann to be a more interesting narrator than the heroic Minke who (in the earlier novels) marries a beautiful half Dutch, half Javanese woman who dies, then he marries a beautiful Chinese revolutionary, who also dies. Both of these deaths he manages to get over. Then there is an engagement to a French Indo girl of seventeen which doesn’t work out, but no matter he then marries a beautiful princess from Maluku. The guy attracts every beautiful woman who graces the pages of the tetralogy! Rejection doesn’t come into it. Who can sympathize with such a character? I’m being facetious, but I really thought Pramoedya's writing here detailing the mental anguish of (the evil) Pangemanann brilliant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13749281910610085832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3462215483062486883">I can say that this is a brilliant book written from Southeast asia. Oh my! I've picked up some words in dutch and Javanese. Can you see how politics and abuse of power work during the colonial period and what more now- these days? This book augurs the present, where we are standing now. <br/><br/>Meneer Pangemanann may be seen as a traitor in the eyes of the natives while, Raden Mas Minke as the unsung hero in the novel, but as a troublemaker in the eyes of the colonial power. However, it is hard to be i</span>
  <span id="freeText3462215483062486883" style="display:none">I can say that this is a brilliant book written from Southeast asia. Oh my! I've picked up some words in dutch and Javanese. Can you see how politics and abuse of power work during the colonial period and what more now- these days? This book augurs the present, where we are standing now. <br /><br />Meneer Pangemanann may be seen as a traitor in the eyes of the natives while, Raden Mas Minke as the unsung hero in the novel, but as a troublemaker in the eyes of the colonial power. However, it is hard to be in the shoes of Meneer Pangemanann. This book does not only analyze the political situation during the Dutch colonialism in Indonesia, but also on the Javanese identities as well as human nature and its problems- greed, prostitution, family problems, alcoholism, corruption, etc.<br /><br />I would like to read the first three books.I started off with the 4th book as it was recommended by my Professor and I do not know there are the other 3 books before House of Glass. Looking forward to read the books. This book is recommended for those who would like to know the past histories of the Republic of Indonesia and this is the best book I ever read, which was from the Southeast Asia. No doubt, Indonesia has a vast literature being written by good authors. I've spent quite a few days to finish reading this book as I was also doing my internet research about what's written by the author, himself. Like, I do not know there was the Chinese Massacre 1740 or also known as the Batavia Massacre. Readers of this books, especially non-Indonesians, will learn a lot from this tiny little novel. (y) </span>

<div id="review_23834788" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23834788" />
    <a title="Henry Wijaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/381136-henry-wijaya"><img alt="Henry Wijaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583913223p2/381136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23834788?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Wijaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Wijaya" href="/user/show/381136-henry-wijaya">Henry Wijaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anybody who loves his or her own country and justice</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Henry by: </span>
      Tym Wise
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23834788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3393404489142064402">This is the last book from the Buru Quartet. This book is absolutely different from the three previous books. It tells the story from the perspective of the officer who trapped, jailed, and at the end "killed" Minke, the main character from the three previous books. From the captain's perspective we can see the incidents happened in and around Minke's life but from outside as the captain's job actually was to observe those who were trying to fight against the colonial government. This kind of ob</span>
  <span id="freeText3393404489142064402" style="display:none">This is the last book from the Buru Quartet. This book is absolutely different from the three previous books. It tells the story from the perspective of the officer who trapped, jailed, and at the end "killed" Minke, the main character from the three previous books. From the captain's perspective we can see the incidents happened in and around Minke's life but from outside as the captain's job actually was to observe those who were trying to fight against the colonial government. This kind of observation was what he called with "rumah kaca" or "the house of glass". Those who were against the government were the objects inside the "glass house" and the captain was the observer. What is interesting to see is actually the captain himself was a secret admirer of Minke who he considered as a very brave local person, a person of honour, a person who had the courage to suffer for the sake of his nation's freedom and betterment. Yet, for the sake of his promotion and position, the captain sacrificed and denied his soul. What else is left for a man who had sold his soul. Nothing but the end.<br />For me, this book teaches us to be brave to defend our own principle and to dare to fight for goodness and justice.<br />An amazing closing for the quartet. I just feel sad with the end of the story because the injustice was somehow not defeated, something that we, unfortunately, always see in our real life...</span>
  <a data-text-id="3393404489142064402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_359100227" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/359100227" />
    <a title="Tse Guang" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7289874-tse-guang"><img alt="Tse Guang" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546158559p2/7289874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/359100227?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tse Guang" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tse Guang" href="/user/show/7289874-tse-guang">Tse Guang</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer359100227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10586381638396042177">I don't have much knowledge of Indonesia and its history, so this review might be totally off, but I found House of Glass nothing short of epic. I'm tempted to take the title as the symbol-key that unlocks the entire work, that reads (neo)colonial power as both all-seeing eye and easily-broken construct. Yet it's so much more than that - Pangemanann's intense admiration for Minke combines with his determination to bring the revolutionary down, producing an intensely ambivalent kind of postcoloni</span>
  <span id="freeText10586381638396042177" style="display:none">I don't have much knowledge of Indonesia and its history, so this review might be totally off, but I found House of Glass nothing short of epic. I'm tempted to take the title as the symbol-key that unlocks the entire work, that reads (neo)colonial power as both all-seeing eye and easily-broken construct. Yet it's so much more than that - Pangemanann's intense admiration for Minke combines with his determination to bring the revolutionary down, producing an intensely ambivalent kind of postcolonial anxiety. <br /><br />Why should I find myself cheering Pangemanann on by the end of the novel? Perhaps because he always saw history as a force mastering men (and not the other way around), because he saw the role Minke played in this process, because he confessed everything to Nyai Ontosoroh. More than that, he took Indonesia and read it like a text, analyzing and interpreting and often getting it right. That Pramoedya's own act of interpretation mirrors his character's is undeniable and revealing. When one can no longer trust the traditions of his own people, and yet cannot uncritically emulate another culture, the Pangemananns and Minkes of the world must decide if they will use their knowledge for selfish or selfless ends. <br /><br />A frightening and brave book, and I'm glad I had the opportunity to read it in the country of its origin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10586381638396042177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('alIe1GKnLv9Wr9q/0otW25HAVzjanqnVOVpV3N951WjpFyTLj1V9bVZ9BJCxOss6eW+GqSv6RCsmL0ImTS7nzw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kexi9KGvUmKnH/QYI2CeGYsd5ZlLeE4r7roiVBCZcjgSqVjrTF0B8KfNKjdA0QP4Y7I0CLoco9XxzzWugs5Anw==')})
  }

She worked as a journalist for Matra, Forum Keadilan, and D&amp;R. Not long after the New Order regime closed Tempo, Editor, and Detik, she participated in the founding of Indonesia’s Alliance of Independent Journalists to protest the closure of those three weeklys. Currently she is working for the cultural journal Kalam, and at Teater Utan Kayu. <span id="freeTextContainer12327411634765668486">Pramoedya Ananta Toer was an Indonesian author of novels, short stories, essays, polemics, and histories of his homeland and its people. A well-regarded writer in the West, Pramoedya's outspoken and often politically charged writings faced censorship in his native land during the pre-reformation era. For opposing the policies of both founding president Sukarno, as well as those of its successor, t</span>
  <span id="freeText12327411634765668486" style="display:none">Pramoedya Ananta Toer was an Indonesian author of novels, short stories, essays, polemics, and histories of his homeland and its people. A well-regarded writer in the West, Pramoedya's outspoken and often politically charged writings faced censorship in his native land during the pre-reformation era. For opposing the policies of both founding president Sukarno, as well as those of its successor, the New Order regime of Suharto, he faced extrajudicial punishment. During the many years in which he suffered imprisonment and house arrest, he became a cause célèbre for advocates of freedom of expression and human rights.<br /><br />Bibliography:<br />* Kranji-Bekasi Jatuh (1947)<br />* Perburuan (The Fugitive) (1950)<br />* Keluarga Gerilya (1950)<br />* Bukan Pasarmalam (1951)<br />* Cerita dari Blora (1952)<br />* Gulat di Jakarta (1953)<br />* Korupsi (Corruption) (1954)<br />* Midah - Si Manis Bergigi Emas (1954)<br />* Cerita Calon Arang (The King, the Witch, and the Priest) (1957)<br />* Hoakiau di Indonesia (1960)<br />* Panggil Aku Kartini Saja I &amp; II (1962)<br />* The Buru Quartet<br /> o Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind) (1980)<br /> o Anak Semua Bangsa (Child of All Nations) (1980)<br /> o Jejak Langkah (Footsteps) (1985)<br /> o Rumah Kaca (House of Glass) (1988)<br />* Gadis Pantai (The Girl from the Coast) (1982)<br />* Nyanyi Sunyi Seorang Bisu (A Mute's Soliloquy) (1995)<br />* Arus Balik (1995)<br />* Arok Dedes (1999)<br />* Mangir (1999)<br />* Larasati (2000)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12327411634765668486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
